type=\"hidden\" value=\"mnras\" /> \r\n    </div>\r\n\r\n                    </div>\r\n                </div>\r\n            </div>\r\n            <div id=\"ContentTab\" class=\"content active\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\r\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082667 id=\"17082667\" class=\"abstract-title js-splitscreen-abstract-title\" >ABSTRACT</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The measurement of the intrinsic shape and orientation of dark matter (DM) and intracluster (IC) gas in galaxy clusters is crucial for constraining their formation and evolution, and for enhancing the use of clusters as more precise cosmological probes. Extending our previous works, for the first time we present the results from a triaxial joint analysis of the galaxy cluster Abell 1835, using X-ray, strong lensing (SL) and Sunyaev\u2013Zel'dovich (SZ) data. We parametrically reconstruct the full three-dimensional structure (triaxial shape and principal axis orientation) of both the DM and the IC gas, and the level of non-thermal pressure of the IC gas. We find that the intermediate\u2013major and minor\u2013major axial ratios of the DM are 0.71 \u00b1 0.08 and 0.59 \u00b1 0.05, respectively, and that the major axis of the DM halo is inclined with respect to the line of sight at 18.3 \u00b1 5.2 deg. We present the first observational measurement of the non-thermal pressure out to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. This has been evaluated to be a few per cent of the total energy budget in the internal regions, while it reaches approximately 20 per cent in the outer volumes. We discuss the implications of our method for the viability of the cold dark matter (CDM) scenario, focusing on the concentration parameter <em>C</em> and the inner slope of the DM \u03b3 in order to test the CDM paradigm for structure formation. We measure \u03b3 = 1.01 \u00b1 0.06 and <em>C</em> = 4.32 \u00b1 0.44; these values are close to the predictions of the CDM model. The combination of X-ray/SL data at high spatial resolution, which are capable of resolving the cluster core, with the SZ data, which are more sensitive to the cluster outer volume, allows us to characterize the level and the gradient of the gas entropy distribution and non-thermal pressure out to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. Thus, we break the degeneracy among the physical models describing the thermal history of the intracluster medium.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix at-ArticleMetadata\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: clusters: general&quot;\">galaxies: clusters: general</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: clusters: individual: Abell 1835&quot;\">galaxies: clusters: individual: Abell 1835</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;cosmic background radiation&quot;\">cosmic background radiation</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;cosmology: observations&quot;\">cosmology: observations</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;X-rays: galaxies: clusters&quot;\">X-rays: galaxies: clusters</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082669 id=\"17082669\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">With the use of the cold dark matter (CDM) paradigm, there has been remarkable success in predicting the large-scale distribution of matter in the Universe, as well as its observed evolution from the earliest epochs to the present day. A fundamental prediction of <em>N</em>-body simulations is that CDM haloes follow a self-similar density profile, with the logarithmic slope of the dark matter (DM) \u03b3 following a shallow power law at small radii (\u03b3 \u223c 1), which then steepens at larger radii (Navarro, Frenk &amp; White <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). However, there is still no comprehensive physical explanation for the origin of such a profile. Moreover, in recent years, there has been much interest in possible discrepancies that remain for the observed and predicted inner density profiles of structures (Limousin et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Sand et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib60\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib60\" data-open=\"bib60\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Newman et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib51\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib51\" data-open=\"bib51\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this perspective, clusters are the optimal place to test the predictions of cosmological simulations regarding the mass profile of dark haloes, and to cast light, in general, on the viability of the standard cosmological framework, which consists of a cosmological constant and cold dark matter (\u039bCDM) with Gaussian initial conditions, by comparing the measured and predicted physical parameters (e.g. the concentration parameter, the inner slope of DM). For example, observations based on a combination of strong lensing (SL) and stellar kinematics have yielded flat inner slopes (\u03b3 \u2243 0.5) for two well-studied clusters (MS2137-23 and Abell 383; Sand et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib60\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib60\" data-open=\"bib60\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Also, Newman et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib51\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib51\" data-open=\"bib51\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) have derived a shallow cusp with \u03b3 &lt; 0.3 (68 per cent) for Abell 611, raising doubts about the predictions of the CDM scenario. Other studies have led to large scatter in the value of \u03b3 from one cluster to another, but these determinations customarily rely on the standard spherical modelling of galaxy clusters. The possible elongation/flattening of the sources along the line of sight, as well as the degeneracy of \u03b3 with other parameters (i.e. the concentration parameter and the scale radius) are likely to affect the estimated values of \u03b3 (Morandi, Pedersen &amp; Limousin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We can also use clusters as an optimal tool to constrain the cosmological parameters, provided that we can accurately determine their mass. For example, a comparison of the cluster baryon fraction <em>f</em><sub>b</sub> with the cosmic baryon fraction can provide a direct constraint on the mean mass density of the Universe, \u03a9<sub>m</sub> (Ettori et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), while the evolution of the cluster mass function can tightly constrain \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> and the dark energy equation of state parameter <em>w</em> (Mantz et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Cluster mass profiles can be probed using several independent techniques, which rely on different physical mechanisms and require different assumptions.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">So far, the analyses of the cluster X-ray/Sunyaev\u2013Zel'dovich (SZ) emission and of the gravitational lensing effect have been among the most promising techniques with which to estimate galaxy cluster masses. Concerning the former, the cluster mass can be measured by studying the intracluster (IC) gas emission under the assumption of hydrostatic equilibrium (HE; see Sarazin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib63\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib63\" data-open=\"bib63\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Indeed, the IC gas emits via both thermal bremsstrahlung in the X-ray band and inverse Compton with the photons of the cosmic microwave background (CMB) spectrum, a process known as the SZ effect (Sunyaev &amp; Zel'dovich <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib73\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib73\" data-open=\"bib73\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). The X-ray mass estimate is less biased compared with the SZ and lensing-derived masses with regard to projection effects, because the emission is traced by the square of the gas density.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The advantage of the SZ effect compared with X-ray emission is the possibility of exploring clusters at higher redshift, because of the absence of cosmological dimming. Moreover, because the SZ intensity depends linearly on the density, unlike the density-squared dependence of the X-ray flux, with the SZ effect it is possible to study clusters without the systematic errors caused by the presence of subclumps and gas in the multiphase state. Also, we can study the physics of the intracluster medium (ICM) well beyond the regions constrained by X-ray observations (\u22640.3\u20130.5 <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">On the other hand, the gravitational lensing effect allows for the determination of the projected surface mass density of the lens, regardless of its dynamical state and independent of the assumption of HE (Miralda-Escude &amp; Babul <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Unfortunately, in most cosmological applications, the projected mass is not the interesting quantity. Rather, we need to measure the three-dimensional mass profile, customarily by assuming spherical symmetry. Lensing mass measurements are also appreciably prone to contamination from foreground and background sources.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Knowledge of the intrinsic shape and orientation of haloes is crucial in order to obtain unbiased determinations of their masses, the inner slope of the DM and the concentration parameter via, for example, X-ray, SZ and lensing data. From this perspective, clusters are commonly modelled as spherical systems whose IC gas is in strict HE (i.e. the equilibrium gas pressure is provided entirely by thermal pressure). These assumptions are only rough approximations, leading to large biases in the determination of the cluster mass and hence on the desired cosmological parameters. Indeed, <em>N</em>-body simulations indicate that DM haloes are triaxial with intermediate-major and minor-intermediate axial ratios, typically of the order of \u223c0.8 (Shaw et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib67\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib67\" data-open=\"bib67\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Wang &amp; White <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib79\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib79\" data-open=\"bib79\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Moreover, hydrodynamical numerical simulations suggest that the plasma in apparently relaxed systems might also be affected by additional non-equilibrium processes, which serve to boost the total pressure, and therefore cause an underestimate of the cluster mass from X-ray/SZ observations (Ameglio et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Lau, Kravtsov &amp; Nagai <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Meneghetti et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">On the observational side, only a few authors have tried to infer the shape or orientation of single objects (Oguri et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib54\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib54\" data-open=\"bib54\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Corless, King &amp; Clowe <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Mahdavi &amp; Chang <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Morandi &amp; Limousin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), and the non-thermal pressure support (Mahdavi et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Sanders, Fabian &amp; Smith <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Richard et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Morandi &amp; Limousin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Using a joint X-ray and lensing analysis, Morandi et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), Morandi, Pedersen &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) overcame the limitations of the standard spherical modelling and strict HE assumption, in order to infer the desired three-dimensional shape and physical properties of galaxy clusters in a bias-free way. A triaxial joint analysis relying on independent and multifrequency data sets for galaxy clusters can relax the assumptions customarily adopted in the cluster analysis, and it can give us additional insights into the underlying physics of these objects.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Extending the findings of our previous works, in this paper we recover the full triaxiality of both the DM and the ICM (i.e. ellipsoidal shape and principal axis orientation) and the level and the gradient of non-thermal pressure for the galaxy cluster Abell 1835. This cluster is a luminous cool-core galaxy cluster at <em>z</em> = 0.253 and it is an optimal candidate for a triaxial joint analysis using X-ray, SZ and lensing techniques, because of its very relaxed dynamical appearance and its exceptional SL system. We discuss the implications of our findings for the viability of the CDM scenario, focusing on the concentration parameter and the inner slope of the DM.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Because of the availability of SZ data out to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, we can infer the properties of the ICM in the outskirts of the galaxy cluster. An accurate measurement of the properties of galaxy clusters out to large radii can provide critical insight into the physics of the ICM and it offers a direct probe of the assembly history of structure formation on the largest scales. Also, it enhances the use of clusters as cosmological probes, because the physics of the IC gas in the outer volumes is relatively simple and nearly self-similar. In particular, we compare our findings with the results of hydrodynamical numerical simulations for the density, temperature, entropy and non-thermal pressure profiles out to the virial radius.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><!----></a></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Throughout this paper, we assume a flat \u039bCDM cosmology; the matter density parameter has the value \u03a9<sub>m</sub> = 0.3, the cosmological constant density parameter is \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.7 and the Hubble constant is <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> = 70 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. At the cluster redshift and for the assumed cosmological parameters, 1 arcsec is equivalent to 3.9 kpc. Unless otherwise stated, quoted errors are at the 68.3 per cent confidence level.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082681 id=\"17082681\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 STRONG LENSING MODELLING</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In a forthcoming publication, we will present a detailed mass model of Abell 1835 and a description of the data used. We summarize it in this section.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082683 id=\"17082683\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>2.1 Multiple images</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The last SL model of Abell 1835 was presented by Richard et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Since then, Abell 1835 has been observed with the Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3) onboard the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) in the F110W and F160W filters (programme 10591; PI, Kneib). These new imaging data have allowed the identification of new multiply imaged systems in the cluster core. Moreover, a spectroscopic campaign that targeted multiple images was carried out using the focal reducer/low dispersion spectrograph 2 (FORS2) on the Very Large Telescope (VLT; programme 087.A-0326; PI, Jullo). This yielded a spectroscopic measurement for some multiple images.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The model presented by Richard et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) was based on seven multiply imaged systems, two of which have a measured spectroscopic redshift. In this paper, the model is based on eight multiply imaged systems, six of which are spectroscopically confirmed. For the remaining systems, the redshifts are allowed to be free during the optimization. These images are listed in Table <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#t1\" class=\"figlink\">1</a></sup> and they are shown in Fig. <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#fig001\" class=\"figlink\">001</a></sup>.</p>                        <a id=\"17082687\" scrollto-destination=\"17082687\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"t1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Table 1</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-tbl001.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=GN6DeDDIP7ZPNDyg4r0Q6g1dADuZ7SU20sV45oqPEWh66VWL-fs8Y-uvCkVKXz4Gsy6yLbQNjRPy5VjOJY-Bt4qfR56BU9G01vVPjsTYn6UbbH683Ao6-0vGiw00yuKtGnTJhpfn8tBHoLN0y5ngqirUXbuGhs9QV1WscgEh3~r0i-ZnQGphe1qWAJlJNuey94fQ53lFudqRhL4QGS61adDhIiorbLS7UMTYJQMYJZZULMVhFXKm3fZ-r3objCtZAMXijt7bQ9ZqAarj0M8KCJ5rqspKHueNx-z2ciNaRSfF6~mABe~LRg6AZU2EQ~m0tWr-Jp7mxeBYdVQIRlEQIQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The multiply imaged systems considered in this paper. The size of the field equals 82 \u00d7 82 arcsec<sup>2</sup>, corresponding to 286 \u00d7 286 kpc<sup>2</sup>. The multiply imaged systems used in this paper are labelled.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082689 id=\"17082689\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>2.2 Mass distribution</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The model of the cluster mass distribution comprises three mass components, described using a dual pseudo-isothermal elliptical mass distribution (dPIE; Limousin, Kneib &amp; Natarajan <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), parametrized by a fiducial velocity dispersion \u03c3, a core radius <em>r</em><sub>core</sub> and a scale radius <em>r</em><sub>s</sub>: (i) a cluster-scale DM halo; (ii) the stellar mass in the brightest cluster galaxy (BCG); (iii) the cluster galaxies representing local perturbation. As in earlier works (e.g. Limousin et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), empirical relations (without any scatter) are used to relate their dynamical dPIE parameters (central velocity dispersion and scale radius) to their luminosity (the core radius being set to a vanishing value, 0.05 kpc), whereas all geometrical parameters (centre, ellipticity and position angle) are set to the values measured from the light distribution. Because they are close to multiple images, following Richard et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), two cluster galaxies are modelled individually, namely P1 and P2 (Fig. <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#fig001\" class=\"figlink\">001</a></sup>). Their scale radii and velocity dispersions are optimized individually. We allow the velocity dispersion of the cluster galaxies to vary between 100 and 250 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, whereas the scale radius is forced to be less than 70 kpc in order to account for the tidal stripping of their DM haloes (e.g. Limousin et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Natarajan et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib47\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib47\" data-open=\"bib47\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Wetzel &amp; White <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, and references therein).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Concerning the cluster-scale DM halo, we set its scale radius to 1000 kpc because we do not have data to constrain this parameter.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The optimization is performed in the image plane, using the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenstool</span><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><!----></a> software (Jullo et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082693 id=\"17082693\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>2.3 Strong lensing results</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The rms in the image plane is equal to 1.4 arcsec. In good agreement with Richard et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), we find that Abell 1835 is well described by a unimodal mass distribution. We note that the galaxy-scale perturbers all present a scale radius that is smaller than the scale radius inferred for isolated field galaxies, in agreement with the tidal stripping scenario.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The <span class=\"small-caps\">lenstool</span> software does explore the parameter space using a Monte Carlo Markov chain (MCMC) sampler. At the end of the optimization, we have access to these MCMC realizations from which we can draw statistics and estimate error bars. For each realization, we build a two-dimensional mass map. All these mass maps are then used to compute the mean mass map and the corresponding covariance matrix. Note that we have checked that the probability distribution function (PDF) of the mass in each pixel is approximately Gaussian. This information is then used in the joint fit.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082696 id=\"17082696\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6>3 X-RAY DATA SETS AND ANALYSIS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The cluster Abell 1835 is a luminous cluster at redshift <em>z</em> = 0.253, which exhibits several indications of a well-relaxed dynamical state; for instance, its X-ray emission peak is associated with a cool core and it is well centred on the BCG. The X-ray isophotes appear to be regular, with a low degree of ellipticity, and with the absence of evident substructures. The global (cooling-core corrected) temperature <em>T</em><sub>ew</sub> has been estimated to be <em>T</em><sub>ew</sub> = 9.38 \u00b1 0.11 keV and the abundance has been evaluated in 0.48 \u00b1 0.03 solar value (Section <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec7\"></span><a href=\"#sec7\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>). We classify this cluster as a strong cooling-core source (SCC; Morandi &amp; Ettori <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>); that is, the central cooling time <em>t</em><sub>cool</sub> is less than the age of the Universe <em>t</em><sub>age, <em>z</em></sub> at the cluster redshift (<em>t</em><sub>cool</sub>/<em>t</em><sub>age, <em>z</em></sub> &lt; 0.1). We have estimated <em>t</em><sub>cool</sub> \u2243 1 \u00d7 10<sup>9</sup> yr. As with other SCCs, Abell 1835 shows a low central temperature (\u223c5 keV) and a strong spike of luminosity in the brightness profile. The temperature profile is very regular, as expected for relaxed clusters (see the upper panel of Fig. <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#fig002\" class=\"figlink\">002</a></sup>).</p>                        <a id=\"17082698\" scrollto-destination=\"17082698\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig002\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-fig002.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=I8IHP8lGXt1KYGfOfUpV~fTeaQIR9a0V9nXxI4RhbsXtyBS5-quHGqQdCnDhDw2pnCd0F7kTbbA~RPsM4q6HQRWeNvMBWKmZLKKsh88sCf7jlzfoTb1SeNgDukE4SZ3fbCbXM3YTdVdtWsS5wjAzmqiOsUr7X~rCjdLJsoqq~xRyhrWeGmn40iEdUIBR6SIR9FGl4fRIXp6j5aRoOaHSTiYaYW6Zqg7jz1uwKj0BLKaDDRI8Gmo~YCotFujT5kM9bJQkgneSWcd9nBhk6iQ4TNY9TdMcqwjbL~XSsYXXfTlCLS9GLBrG4UCgQuaQlKX1g9C0Yz9r-xW4OQzuV5ny7g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Example of the joint analysis of T, SX, \u0394TSZ(v) and \u03a3. Here, we only briefly summarize the most relevant or novel aspects of our data reduction and analysis of Abell 1835.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082700 id=\"17082700\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec7>3.1 X-ray data reduction</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We have reduced the <em>Chandra</em> X-ray data using the <span class=\"small-caps\">ciao</span> data analysis package, version 4.3, and the calibration data base CALDB 4.4.3. Here, we briefly summarize the reduction procedure. We have performed our X-ray analysis on four data sets retrieved from the National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA) HEASARC archive (observation IDs 6880, 6881, 7370 and 496) with a total exposure time of approximately 200 ks. Three observations (IDs 6880, 6881 and 7370) have been carried out using the Advanced CCD Imaging Spectrometer I-array (ACIS-I) and telemetered in the very faint mode and one observation (ID 496) has been carried out using the ACIS-S and telemetered in the faint mode (ID 2321). The level-1 event files were reprocessed to apply the appropriate gain maps and calibration products and to reduce the ACIS quiescent background. We used the acis_process_events tool to check for cosmic ray background events and to correct for eventual spatial gain variations caused by charge transfer inefficiency in order to recompute the event grades. Then, we filtered the data to include the standard events grades 0, 2, 3, 4 and 6 only, and therefore we have filtered for the good time intervals (GTIs) supplied, which are contained in the flt1.fits file. The bright point sources were identified and masked out using the script vtpdetect, and the result was then checked by visual inspection.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Then we used the tool dmextract to create the light curve of the background. Indeed, a careful screening of the background light curve is necessary for a correct background subtraction and to discard contaminating flare events. In order to clean the data sets of periods of anomalous background rates, we used the deflare script, so as to filter out the times where the background count rate exceeds \u00b13\u03c3 of the mean value. Finally, we filtered the ACIS event files on energy, by selecting the range 0.3\u201312 keV, and on CCDs, so as to obtain a level-2 event file.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082703 id=\"17082703\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec8>3.2 X-ray spatial and spectral analysis</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We measure the gas density profile from the surface brightness recovered by a spatial analysis, and we infer the projected temperature profile by analysing the spectral data.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The X-ray images were extracted from the level-2 event files in the energy range (0.5\u20135.0 keV), corrected by the exposure map to remove the vignetting effects. Point sources were then masked and the images were rebinned by a factor of 4 (1 pixel = 1.968 arcsec).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We determined the centroid (<em>x</em><sub>c</sub>, <em>y</em><sub>c</sub>) of the surface brightness by locating the position where the <em>X</em> and <em>Y</em> derivatives go to zero, which is usually a robust and outlier-resistance approach. We have checked that the X-ray emission is centred on the BCG; the distance between the X-ray centroid and the BCG centre is \u22431.8 arcsec (the uncertainty on this measure is comparable to the smoothing scale applied to the X-ray image to determine the centroid).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The spectral analysis was performed by extracting the source spectra in circular annuli of radius <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq022.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=g8W9EwOxGGhVb3WDdRQxaeUvTaZ0zup9-2UeDte6w79EOX~wi659P7y3RUKugdksyD5Bk020GsxkiD-f8ijS9TqqW~V39WmhCERDs5-ARibJHIqiW1Y8zMb2S8AG8BSRT6f98qGWeo9ZHVsNJGRrd2V3zDfvPU68-qB-JEhq2VJnCVWniJYB8QrSdF4eXeTA69YnJXCsIgyrTDQIm8-XxktVpOATPQvdyU9dEePrgJxxqbZYQV5FkJ0ub4Rozzp--wEJCT7JcWa4RKuppI6MWBQ4CEGm4zteoYvkTbc-yLX~A7j0U6hrY-mLxueu-N4Vf3bsirSs8Vqk~Da7~INwyQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq022.jpeg\" /> around the X-ray surface brightness centroid. We have selected <em>n</em>* = 8 annuli out to a maximum distance <em>R</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1095 kpc, according to the following criteria. The number of net counts of photons from the source in the band used for the spectral analysis is at least 2000 per annulus, which corresponds to a fraction of the total counts always larger than 30 per cent. We used the <span class=\"small-caps\">ciao</span> specextract tool to extract the source and background spectra and to construct the redistribution matrix files (RMFs) and the ancillary response files (ARFs).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Each of the <em>n</em>* annuli have been analysed using the <span class=\"small-caps\">xspec</span> package (Arnaud <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) by simultaneously fitting an absorbed optically thin plasma emission model (the <span class=\"small-caps\">mekal</span> model; Kaastra <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Liedahl, Osterheld &amp; Goldstein <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) to the four observations. The fit is performed in the energy range 0.6\u20137 keV by fixing the redshift at <em>z</em> = 0.253, and the photoelectric absorption at the galactic value (i.e. to the value inferred from radio H <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> maps). For each of the <em>n</em>* annuli, we grouped the photons into bins of 20 counts per energy channel and we applied \u03c7<sup>2</sup>-statistics. Thus, for each of the annuli, the free parameters in the spectral analysis were the normalization of the thermal spectrum <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq023.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=cG1gYCJgkb-ZvkMVbLJ6~8SXG3JxENC~VznCRQcsd-20LFwb6cSPxn3RjVi0K2POXOJMCvNooaxKRVtAmGF8Typ-FQK-f-K6ctRO2F7kwYZtDWQT8IErazKmDeTvMG89Cdlq8qZzPinC1panxT~ENaKi7QPISsRB3jtFdWomQMeqasblCiTmVYOtNV6pxPb5E7Js-vITsC7JVe0hYTgP0ke8KIBoxvxagHpJK9cbPKIV5Y-Hdu6eORdUf4nfNC39ReGIiWQTHcEtbHmaIy5FsKBR32Hevz2Jrd-iTbydsVUhbgYc1hMfJjGciNuKJKJi3QGg8ws6pLwjETuc1w7u1A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq023.jpeg\" />, the emission-weighted temperature <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq024.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=R4jWfkNyRl14nfceYk1nbdHpKWzjvv6ocVus-0TAo3GJ-nPJZGOgk~UN9qrVbuhsdqVYXVFpLc4BYJod0WF~-vlEYS3Xl8MvRjbxqOn00lEZWAq3Ad7XxLyVLEQep0Ww0zuVDJIqzuFI1CVMfxt3DuaWRAehlNE2f51LhlkrhPbRMKs-wDMfF6~3k01ifE5yTDeLg7YGoCV12FCf6sCGKFt4JrpS7V4GLju~UzvSg4O7BLxSeNHLrxr~lJ775UmK~l2sMofQ0SW31MMWMfzIs6F9nze1-Q0t6Q9JSLaUzbn6RHt6YnE6lISfVw4JZQuLozxitoDU1HxzFjqvoHiP5g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq024.jpeg\" />, and the metallicity <em>Z</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The four observations were first analysed individually, to assess the consistency of the data sets and to exclude any systematic effects that could influence the combined analysis. Then, we proceeded with the joint spectral analysis of the four data sets.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The background spectra have been extracted from regions of the same exposure for the ACIS-I observations, for which we always have some areas free from source emission. We have also checked for systematic errors resulting from possible source contamination of the background regions. Conversely, for the ACIS-S observation, we have considered the ACIS-S3 chip only and we used the ACIS blank-sky background files. We have extracted the blank-sky spectra from the blank-field background data sets provided by the ACIS calibration team in the same chip regions as the observed cluster spectra. The blank-sky observations underwent a reduction procedure comparable to the one applied to the cluster data, after being reprojected on to the sky according to the observation aspect information by using the reproject_events tool. Then, we scaled the blank-sky spectrum level to the corresponding observational spectrum in the 9\u201312 keV interval, because in this band the <em>Chandra</em> effective area is negligible and thus very little cluster emission is expected. One of the advantages of this method is that the derived ARFs and RMFs will be consistent for both the source and the background spectrum. However, the background in the X-ray soft band can vary in both time and space, so it is important to check whether the background derived by the blank-sky data sets is consistent with the real one. From this perspective, we have verified that for the ACIS-I observations the two methods of background subtraction provide very similar results for the fit parameters (e.g. the temperature).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082711 id=\"17082711\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec9>4 SZ DATA SET AND ANALYSIS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The SZ data were collected using Bolocam in 2006, and they have been presented previously in Sayers et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Since that publication, these data have been reduced again using a slightly modified reduction pipeline, which we briefly describe here. First, the flux calibration model has been updated, based on recent <em>Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</em> (<em>WMAP</em>) results, as described in Sayers, Czakon &amp; Golwala (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib65\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib65\" data-open=\"bib65\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). This results in &lt;5 per cent changes to the flux calibration. Secondly, there have been some minor changes to the data-flagging procedures, which, in general, have a very small effect on the final SZ image. Thirdly, we note that the coordinates in the Abell 1835 image thumbnails in Sayers et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) are offset in declination by 1 arcmin 9 arcsec because of a typographical error in the source coordinate file for that cluster. There is no such coordinate offset for any of the other clusters presented in Sayers et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although the full data reduction procedure is described in detail in Sayers et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), we briefly discuss the relevant aspects of this processing here. In particular, the data are effectively high-pass filtered in a complicated and slightly non-linear way in order to subtract noise resulting from fluctuations in the opacity of the atmosphere (i.e. the transfer function of the filtering depends weakly on the cluster profile). We fit an elliptical generalized Navarro\u2013Frenk\u2013White (gNFW) profile (Nagai, Kravtsov &amp; Vikhlinin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Arnaud et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) to Abell 1835, which has provided a good fit to the data (\u03c7<sup>2</sup>/d.o.f. = 964/945). Note that this is slightly different from the value given in table 2 of Sayers et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) (\u03c7<sup>2</sup>/d.o.f. = 966/945), because of the slightly different data flagging used in this analysis. The transfer function computed for this model is then used for all of our subsequent analyses (i.e. all models have been filtered using this transfer function prior to comparing them to the SZ data). We have verified that the biases associated with using this single transfer function are negligible compared to the noise in the image. The effects of this transfer function can be clearly seen in the third panel from the top of Fig. <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#fig002\" class=\"figlink\">002</a></sup>, where a radial profile of the SZ data is plotted.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082714 id=\"17082714\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec10>5 THREE-DIMENSIONAL STRUCTURE OF GALAXY CLUSTERS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The lensing and the X-ray/SZ emission both depend on the properties of the DM gravitational potential well. The former is a direct probe of the two-dimensional mass map via the lensing equation and the latter is an indirect proxy of the three-dimensional mass profile through the HE equation applied to the gas temperature and density. In order to infer the model parameters of both the IC gas and of the underlying DM density profile, we perform a joint analysis of SL and X-ray/SZ data. We briefly outline the methodology in order to infer physical properties in triaxial galaxy clusters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"></p><ul class=\"list-simple\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">We start with a gNFW triaxial model of the DM, as described in Jing &amp; Suto (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), which is representative of the total underlying mass distribution. This depends on a few parameters to be determined: the concentration parameter <em>C</em>; the scale radius <em>R</em><sub>s</sub>; the inner slope of the DM \u03b3; the two axial ratios (\u03b7<sub>DM, a</sub> and \u03b7<sub>DM, b</sub>); the Euler angles \u03c8, \u03b8 and \u03c6. </p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">Following Lee &amp; Suto (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), we recover the gravitational potential and two-dimensional surface mass \u03a3 (equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq011\"></span><a href=\"#eq011\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>) of a DM halo using this triaxial density profile. </p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">We solve the generalized HE equation (i.e. including the non-thermal pressure <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub>; see equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq005\"></span><a href=\"#eq005\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>) for the density of the IC gas sitting in the gravitational potential well previously calculated, in order to infer the theoretical three-dimensional temperature profile <em>T</em>. </p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">We calculate the SZ temperature decrement map \u0394<em>T</em><sub>SZ</sub>(\u03bd) (equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq009\"></span><a href=\"#eq009\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>) and the surface brightness map <em>S</em><sub>X</sub> (equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq008\"></span><a href=\"#eq008\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>) related to the triaxial ICM halo. </p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">The joint comparison of <em>T</em> with the observed temperature, of <em>S</em><sub>X</sub> with the observed brightness image, of \u0394<em>T</em><sub>SZ</sub>(\u03bd) with the observed SZ temperature decrement, and of \u03a3 with the observed two-dimensional mass map give us the parameters of the triaxial ICM and DM density models.</p></li></ul><p class=\"chapter-para\">Here, we briefly summarize the major findings of Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) for the triaxial joint analysis, in order to infer the desired physical properties, as well as the improvements added in the current analysis. Additional details can be found in Morandi, Ettori &amp; Moscardini (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), Morandi et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">In Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), we modelled the DM and ICM ellipsoids to be orientated in a arbitrary direction on the sky. We introduced two Cartesian coordinate systems, <em>x</em> = (<em>x</em>, <em>y</em>, <em>z</em>) and <em>x</em>\u2032 = (<em>x</em>\u2032, <em>y</em>\u2032, <em>z</em>\u2032), which represent respectively the principal coordinate system of the triaxial dark halo and the observer's coordinate system, with the origins set at the centre of the halo. We assumed that the <em>z</em>\u2032-axis lies along the line of sight to the observer and that the <em>x</em>\u2032, <em>y</em>\u2032 axes identify the directions of west and north, respectively, on the plane of the sky. We also assumed that the <em>x</em>, <em>y</em>, <em>z</em>-axes lie along the minor, intermediate and major axes, respectively, of the DM halo. If we define \u03c8, \u03b8 and \u03c6 as the rotation angles about the <em>x</em>, <em>y</em> and <em>z</em> axes, respectively, then the relation between the two coordinate systems can be expressed in terms of the rotation matrices <em>M</em><sub><em>x</em></sub>(\u03c8), <em>M</em><sub><em>y</em></sub>(\u03b8), <em>M</em><sub><em>z</em></sub>(\u03c6) with Euler angles \u03c8, \u03b8, \u03c6: as the major-axis length of the iso-density surfaces: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq004\" content-id=\"eq004\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div eccentricities (e.g. <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq026.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=la6lkcC0dRhGzWdIP4GE8f9gpwrKiAp31jolhcAjoc7PG1KiftWhu2vlDOfAUnsarYcqSAuVTjNozkwrtItp-E3FlJSjndyjCCoJMWb58NaC3WQl1RZSfikWr6xu2FZFnRVReGPtHB~o3SEbohhs-FFCJz982uzT7P~AghhXkQP~X0vCcpjm8MRd3rtY~o2lAmKRdbdIKXzC2ciVdKl0LTK9JjPvM1CqJaNOpNk8omgnnxRjb7GL8jBqTSljXCd223lDFjwfMY3UdvMUPcMBcsRZnV18RRz8EDr1rGIOFEMvVXQ5uU7FQIH6JGP~7FJsa3mRNTZmXIfY69pT62aiUg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq026.jpeg\" />).</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Lee &amp; Suto (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) have shown that the iso-potential surfaces of the triaxial dark halo are well approximated by a sequence of concentric triaxial distributions of radius <em>R</em><sub>icm</sub> with different eccentricity ratios. <em>R</em><sub>icm</sub> has a similar definition as <em>R</em> (equation 4), but with IC gas eccentricities \u03b5<sub>b</sub> and \u03b5<sub>c</sub>. Note that \u03b5<sub>b</sub> = \u03b5<sub>b</sub>(<em>e</em><sub>b</sub>, <em>u</em>, \u03b3) and \u03b5<sub>c</sub> = \u03b5<sub>c</sub>(<em>e</em><sub>c</sub>, <em>u</em>, \u03b3), with <em>u</em> = <em>R</em>/<em>R</em><sub>s</sub>, unlike the constant <em>e</em><sub>b</sub>, <em>e</em><sub>c</sub> for the adopted DM halo profile. In the whole range of <em>u</em>, \u03b5<sub>b</sub>/<em>e</em><sub>b</sub> (\u03b5<sub>c</sub>/<em>e</em><sub>c</sub>) is less than unity (\u223c0.7 at the centre). That is, the IC gas is altogether more spherical than the underlying DM halo (see Morandi et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a> for further details).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082722 id=\"17082722\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec11>5.1 X-ray, SZ and lensing equations</h3>\r\n<div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">For the X-ray analysis, we rely on a generalization of the HE equation (Morandi et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), which accounts for the non-thermal pressure <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub>: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq005\" content-id=\"eq005\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq005.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq005.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=Tpju3024uGbQCnpqChMshlQ-lfTPiZB9c3zucwsoQE3tgfdkyEUItz0bnXeC-HNfiGwycz2b7BO-GLiq4Rv458EAwx6znO2UlNafnR1GBTju6Dsk8cfbHt41fygeWFeI0RovUNlbRvn0yNPXj~zyokySzodDgsZjSayJ-6HLZB1RFf07e-8JkTTI1M2UGvqLYVFxJ5iqCDB9tdGZKsHG9AgOdbSLRIiI1loJHQ2-rUF3TJYcXEjm6D0HROCergzIqQpmYttc1O5tL8aC7sk0pT0r6oR19T8GuU0POCw-1pk82XYCR-TuserLsE3ocyso6C68T-3sz570CVrwCLlAQg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq005.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">5</span></div>Here, \u03c1<sub>gas</sub> is the gas mass density, \u03a6 is the gravitational potential and <em>P</em><sub>tot</sub> = <em>P</em><sub>th</sub> + <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub>. We have implemented a model where <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub> is a fraction of the total pressure <em>P</em><sub>tot</sub>, and we set this fraction to be a power law with radius (Shaw et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>): <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq006\" content-id=\"eq006\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq006.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq006.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=titDi1Se71kN~5MU7-abCrql42yYhFTJamSgTV8OD07c4Hl~uHu-obI-y71W8fS6v~mcOkSkpLVFdZpLF~f~WPPb5sfgVuAY5~2VB8LAO7~9Kh7hsVeFJFfBpOSgsUY8TA9OYnPnSHoOv5yhd8wD8H1yElGQZzVARjFq~ekOywb-XAIgthB2fpKgVBB0Yh~GdHy8pDF8kGD6HpqHU1UOLouU2gvigpcdNoRXbf-EVWzEETDkyTTtrXEWAjIWTt3qauq1FiHFF59sEN3htne4psdp7qZIyWXjABpxJt3s42a-RqLfV7Bk5lxC0U-kHjcljw2eYzZGnWC37O7Yr~VtIg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq006.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">6</span></div>Note that X-ray and SZ data probe only the thermal component of the gas <em>P</em><sub>th</sub> = <em>n</em><sub>e</sub><em>k</em><sub>b</sub><em>T</em>, where <em>k</em><sub>b</sub> is the Boltzmann constant. From equations <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq005\"></span><a href=\"#eq005\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a> and <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq006\"></span><a href=\"#eq006\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>, we can see that neglecting <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub> (i.e. <em>P</em><sub>tot</sub> = <em>P</em><sub>th</sub>) systematically bias low the determination of cluster mass profiles. We stress that the model in equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq006\"></span><a href=\"#eq006\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a> is an improvement with respect to that of Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), where we have assumed that the non-thermal pressure is a constant fraction of the total pressure.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Given that equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq005\"></span><a href=\"#eq005\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a> is a first-order differential equation, we need a boundary condition on the pressure, <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq027.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=w4LJNADsdSnJQW41XgGwxN9AjRvd82pihboIPrQajXcmW3~fN3sAlULz83npu3nf2Gdg8E59Dxf9ifFGc2LnhUuarN-T~DEiEJjMK8smIz6zgMohbVuSQ6XFYasaeLSCH8j~Lr2mBe0H32gL~d7t2lLGmrgoaje5LwGqFAPPj9PyfPPCtJbJDiRsXLJWMqC3SkCrUvfD~R-Ymgsd2bTOMQVw5PrhnIpbxZ-RDbQ2FOG0ra33LepgHMB8FcDzlAFEN2AkFkQmuxYgCt~9CtWDxU2Skn0J9EQT9cMUe6U1DoLH~Yunt9xg--14K2ZP3PJZQT-ndCNCROW4N4T5scJK6Q__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq027.jpeg\" />, which represents the pressure at <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, and this is an unknown parameter to be determined.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">To model the electron density profile in the triaxial ICM halo, we use the following fitting function <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq007\" content-id=\"eq007\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq007.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq007.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=bpiE72wX1buBEqrd7-wnpY~UUVAvllXkaWY1CmWOzELl5TawdRV2l9icipp7L1w9p6qwAEkMRVgg773YM4qW33aN6tKfniL7xUlneovWsYg7pxfVwIVeXVR99MpBD~Gc7mQ9-8aZ0a25RPPJI9ZfORVAKA8qZHKmlUV2Cj40MdwipSBHeOKJvPtZWRcCePUE3YGlFlUt2I3XTSZNy1ijs76dSFS--jGS1Iq8G0gKZZjBWuSk~MocPolvCWq0Lue66MQqwLlMAXSBZRrGWpVoL9-YTyLuWkZfWLbfCBCN--wh~o8yXuKQfuK2Af4STefLm3S1Cg2fMQ5~YYfuJKr9og__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq007.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">7</span></div>with parameters (<img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq028.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=rRJzg91jOv26~k7~QwT9HsZWgUwQKDmT1TwwZRJ-rBa~zLUKnctzbfDPVZsLdUtmS1YjLWrMLnXGQSmZipjpiQt7xewtyM577lvzTe9x63n9qgsqW4-EpqxhUskUm0~A1HSQqIjYLmaDr83Bpv12BT0WGEztwsZeAKkPqCToR89OWVRjzJTkFJLkWVRaSNw-JS40Da8paQa2~OEN7LX-Nj2aw4G6lWmf8UXAk9HeZRnUidrkK86b~aqmJf8THGk8lN3U0sOlm3a0PXS9tptNm0XNHH0cdEqVvxxxBD35eZaY~10p6D7lcAfUAeX62d6-a1awp6UsD1j248yQW~ff-g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq028.jpeg\" />). Note that the fitting function in equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq007\"></span><a href=\"#eq007\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a> has more degrees of freedom than that employed in Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). We have computed the theoretical three-dimensional temperature <em>T</em> by numerically integrating the equation of the HE (equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq005\"></span><a href=\"#eq005\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>), assuming triaxial geometry and a functional form of the gas density given by equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq007\"></span><a href=\"#eq007\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The observed X-ray surface brightness <em>S</em><sub>X</sub> is given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq008\" content-id=\"eq008\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq008.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq008.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=n4ubwiZMsyk5EWzUuzdAJ8DQJAK0hdd9LwpqJ~qzXoDL0U1ox6NyfNQwh31NM9vdEsDbqZQZfYOae~6Wkrf2yYxSb-xpIbzKO9ByNQb~7GpnjUxyILT2Xm3Az83SsEtpldA3~BrZ-to8uZ1mOa7E~gslQbmkTCJnDq9PQK~~My0VBXBfk436PuAVl7tw~4nQK8~PB8C3KAZyl48jaLy4Uv2jikEB9mTz65LkyrC4vB~MuUcn2QHIWSUSjSFmq~mpCIe0Z1aVQumH6S9t18it5c90VKFDLdTCvc8xLOrNnKdNY8aWBT3jta06QDMe8P2UeXmjbRt5CsHIKSRoTUuMJw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq008.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">8</span></div>where <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq029.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=irZzm4FRG4U4YdoT96ANcGZJHUpV4VACG5xmfdIjPESJUpOn6PNLluM2BQGdh1TYKid~XDHTUyC7yhL7Y5r27dbCETWm1MW9eVrjuiueBml4o944vK3zVViv2q9utF~Ud~9V0A1YiZMNUrO1rI7uJX1qPusEbRo6uktQlonS7o~j-pMsqCxqkiF3BG2Pub2uz2ZKAaPoEpfJRz430C-o6xhrJrWG-sQvYSzOP3OVgvLbyTiQ-qDnHGIMuxl6JBG6uKZsV5Pmu~ysCAeDr4JfWvI0Sx62rT23-FjlJEZ~sTb9POKxti7gMzoy4YFAXZl98YXDh6ilPk9wQY5G1tkXnA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq029.jpeg\" /> is the cooling function. Because the projection on the sky of the plasma emissivity gives the X-ray surface brightness, the latter can be geometrically fitted with the model <em>n</em><sub>e</sub>(<em>R</em><sub>icm</sub>) of the assumed distribution of the electron density (equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq007\"></span><a href=\"#eq007\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>) by applying equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq008\"></span><a href=\"#eq008\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>. This has been accomplished by using fake <em>Chandra</em> spectra, where the current model is folded through response curves (ARFs and RMFs) and then it is added to a background file, with absorption, temperature and metallicity measured in the neighbouring ring in the spectral analysis (Section <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec8\"></span><a href=\"#sec8\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>). In order to calculate <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq030.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=rBLataiOvgrFqbSIHAOyA8T-PASYTMjdnX4~Yx4AE1xyg8Dftnm2Ecrnr41DmdZB4Gn8r0hUMd2Ym~JumQzWqin9o1~SS1uvL-WSsV-xQRyV~XEhxEpMxrR3tYw7I5D4gSnrT8y942YaMtYcUVK-h8jW5U63d51AYmz0VKjUw~XCl2aes743xw58AflzmQKJ5Fn-q6nKKKjiBrNBLXWCNQodtspR4GQu7-JJvOkKb5lzE5mLKTM4SjU-Tztc3eeDiofoVsFgtyw2x3-FtxxhJt27z0Yzfy5Jtdiqix7yYUqWIEYkjcUB~C0MrSGmJt6qnZqjIJqYlLDlv7OGr2Kb6w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq030.jpeg\" />, we have adopted a <span class=\"small-caps\">mekal</span> model (Kaastra <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Liedahl et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) for the emissivity.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The thermal SZ effect is expressed as a small variation in the temperature \u0394<em>T</em><sub>SZ</sub>(\u03bd) of the CMB as a function of the observation frequency: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq009\" content-id=\"eq009\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq009.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq009.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=P2TQNc9261bpLcdcjTEN4vkZqR0KoHULUMwNzAWi3ElBNmVewP9eaA9Igd8VEVJKhgNlKK3MUQ3M6A1RFsZvl1dMMsM6TPQpU8BsKOr4llorw4Hmd45i8OUsMlTXiYHmZ4tJP2BEzDAUCHES9PxpKQnbDGDLt~t7wgjLz5YgCKuuS-lZipLixp1wteZ9C4qxzXXajPJAQkbxpwbcdSx3kx~j~7r5c4Z4yYkd2dTqp7njagOEjtnJiXYyFQO48FokZBE6oMrSaAwGYjcSAeFf-KdbEUDX0k1nnDmY3ZayPkalOPO0R67onLQSyonI-xHKOMzwPN52xGEJ8Hop2LZfwA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq009.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">9</span></div>Here, \u03c3<sub>T</sub> is the Thomson cross-section, <em>P</em><sub>e</sub>(<em>r</em>) \u2261 <em>n</em><sub>e</sub>(<em>r</em>)<em>k</em><sub><em>b</em></sub><em>T</em>(<em>r</em>) is the pressure of the electrons of the ICM at the volume element of coordinate <em>r</em>, <em>k</em><sub>b</sub> is the Boltzmann constant and <em>T</em><sub>cmb</sub> = 2.725 K.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \"><em>f</em>[\u03bd; <em>T</em>(<em>r</em>)] takes into account the spectral shape of the SZ effect, as follows <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq010\" content-id=\"eq010\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq010.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq010.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=doQzdYGpoG9qKPDmm~lliMlzF7-2xUsA8KyyMmGn1Jc9~2Ib5sbLwuB07WYqkNsZcyHfuyoPf4kKDTB0UZ8-ZuaHAKiRc10Lb74tDY2uvbT7I6MhYVYwtko-cIQrZPkOzIQp1jqylP3hQF3xxrb~VMFY0dBUI83bB4kPY-66V~4tz8Eo~wxyQ-UbBqJ9zPympPBf~HxiLJWrxyndB7MKxMr9B1A80IiggcbOHV7OEDk0ZuU-1hgWVrfT4xdoEM5vG790J~cMspXkr~BFOLLJKF1q0fXIB9XDqu1qm4CAtaLqUtXYfBaT93YmHBGH-HCH9OSRkZ1rd5I4Jbl1SQnhww__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq010.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">10</span></div>Here, <em>x</em> = <em>h</em>\u03bd/<em>kT</em><sub>cmb</sub> accounts for the frequency dependence of the SZ effect, and for the relativistic corrections related to the term <em>o</em><sub><em>f</em></sub>(<em>x</em>, <em>T</em>) (Itoh, Kohyama &amp; Nozawa <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Note that, in equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq009\"></span><a href=\"#eq009\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>, we account for the implicit dependence of <em>f</em>[\u03bd; <em>T</em>(<em>r</em>)] on radius.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Next, the two-dimensional SZ model \u0394<em>T</em><sub>SZ</sub>(\u03bd) is convolved with the Bolocam point spread function and the measured transfer function. In practice, the transfer function convolution is performed by multiplication in the Fourier domain. This filtering significantly reduces the peak decrement of the cluster and it creates a ring of positive flux at <em>r</em> \u223c 2 arcmin. This filtered model is then compared to the observed SZ temperature decrement map. We have also calculated the noise covariance matrix <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq031.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=aM1al2sNNvwKk78CqILboZbN5xV11iaEhx933US7q6FFGboXuCojpUvsLVcszyamywtjV45kd96ujvmOI3kzQCsjMMFAYp6vveJxMRE~50Le4gHX10knl89F5H~ayVZiW404C003ZF4MFDfUGLSTtvtJFzcotUjVQb~0OI3-WqSqSAyllA5d34yyKugoNAgrtT5yyo9U8cY~enWXdfCp7dgzz3ikUqbRGCgVlMmf05vJ512AEiMk~6BVGyuhlW0pzXvyMI32wezUiW~Mwvcx-Hq9nxcmL8oR7-~M55UQtGu6HkUfVb16jojgpVIQf~F-6ld8kW3C~G45Cvia4M2nVA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq031.jpeg\" /> among all the pixels of the observed SZ temperature decrement map through 1000 jack-knife realizations of our cluster noise. In this perspective, we have assumed that the noise covariance matrix for the SZ data is diagonal, because this was shown to be a good assumption in Sayers et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">For the lensing analysis, the two-dimensional surface mass density \u03a3 can be expressed as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq011\" Given that the number of counts in each bin might be small (&lt;5), we cannot assume that the Poisson distribution from which the counts are sampled has a nearly Gaussian shape. The standard deviation (i.e. the square-root of the variance) for this low-count case has been derived by Gehrels (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq019\" content-id=\"eq019\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq019.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq019.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=GEAR8rgXDWwsZC6nIEGSGiICwNDI1lVKxmVzrHLA9zxcQrRmgKSxHXgVjcPa6ZlS6wfJlOz8mUanaOed1RDLAVMjAWL~SVx~Zbqd6INLpTewvRiRRl2WgUa5rvd3MonZfWO1kAFP7FWqEdZZcZCcpUE-0iit40dGf1xpKqecXfjX~mQ07FAUzAcwLIetN3faAVRfwiMIZrtmfC8bpRNxEFqYhWn~YWcz2RNhdjadTMIsVfcaHKhmtp35Wa972yLsEFhvYntFyRWTQh43MGtHS7BoNB4NUn9b67ycGsaYhIYJebzC98b4zyIqdytQa~GbEQKbC2SDNOpqbhF6hwShag__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq019.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">16</span></div>which has been demonstrated to be accurate to approximately 1 per cent. Note that we have added background to <em>S</em><sub>X, <em>i</em></sub> as measured locally in the brightness images, and that vignetting has been removed in the observed brightness images.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">For the SZ (lensing) constraint <em>D</em>, the <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq020.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq020.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=HhtYbuaBkQXNkCeIrl7Ec9Fv2d9b5e8DBQ3k8RAHR9LgMDZq6XXN4AddxxcHwYgKo3zu6ZElFR0MlHfTBnP0lU7JKa1lgNe4D05z9TcKHzuQYYt~cE2lvipDFnFPP7Vj955yqD0RO7rTW02BwxEHwEEfo3C4Di7ahuqMwN-4GvzIew1WrihufXUCgjeJ7vn8xU-ozVMCcRoQIVyJvaiFHuiwQHcQbtavygXTOGUH8k~kn7njCV9c1KCHBzKGb7fIx8qATAhEa7Uz1h37Fq9dzLc-Av36ctDvqrt6iyvAfthEwuuccedJFJ~5TijJKYOZTjDotZguBCk8fWTJuMQ5og__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq020.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">17</span></div>where <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq047.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=zH7qkXuU6qHJDiXY2rPes9pSUTE8pGnEBVhCngo6TcoMpfwSnmuU3wQ6ihqMbeKD59w6g5PYIUl9sgYB56~JSUS~i0ueCMnvasmuWreQlGowUpdDtI4DVfEDQDvqEMPCL2m9VJ2PV~3AUKS9V0QAAYNUxItdENyjcnq5wJqbl~3zqV~Iipr0kIh0QCNDwBp0vnQrE2gzilcZg4WlnibsTS0sAe7cKR9cokuqDJuWVbfWyxFRXlfiKKyluRBxnZKtXUqoxLybWsrGmriF8-FLlKDY-Fmpt1k8rnqGA~T97p8jjeKIvWk2dfFtFODA19dz3k6CO2IDhYc05WjIA2aaWw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq047.jpeg\" /> is the covariance matrix of the two-dimensional SZ temperature decrement (projected mass), <em>D</em>* are the observed measurements of the two-dimensional SZ temperature decrement (projected mass) in the <em>i</em>th pixel and <em>D</em> is the theoretical two-dimensional model. Note that we have removed the central 25 kpc of the two-dimensional projected mass in the joint analysis, to avoid contamination from the central dominant (cD) galaxy mass.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We report the average value and standard deviation of the marginal probability distribution for the individual parameters. In addition to a complete statistical analysis of the chain, we have performed a series of convergence tests: <em>R</em> statistics of Gelman &amp; Rubin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and a split-test, which essentially consists of splitting the chain into two, three or four parts and comparing the difference in the parameter quantiles. We have confirmed the convergence of our result using these tests.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Thus we can determine the physical parameters of the cluster (e.g. the three-dimensional temperature <em>T</em>, or the shape of the DM and the ICM) by relying on the generalized HE equation and on the robust results of the hydrodynamical simulations of the DM profiles (i.e. gNFW). We should point out that, given the complementary data sets that have been included in this analysis, we do not need to rely on any prior from theoretical predictions, such as priors on the concentration parameter, on the halo mass or on the axial ratios (e.g. Corless et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), which might be biased because of our incomplete understanding of the cluster physics in simulations.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig. <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#fig002\" class=\"figlink\">002</a></sup> presents an example of a joint analysis for <em>T</em>, <em>S</em><sub>X</sub>, \u0394<em>T</em><sub>SZ</sub>(\u03bd) and \u03a3; for <em>S</em><sub>X</sub>, \u0394<em>T</em><sub>SZ</sub>(\u03bd) and \u03a3, the one-dimensional profile has been presented only for visualization purposes, with the fit applied to the two-dimensional X-ray brightness/SZ/surface mass data. Note that in the joint analysis the X-ray, SZ and lensing data are all well described by our model, with <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq048.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=qBdINpKfeB~4TIvxry4BJDhP~mVPlepfu1Kwr74lQ1Gj6sxCQ0zmhuViJmoyBJEA1abL5rImkRTqulXBbUS7~PyNJ5LE-loRD1kbXDrtXZnQrVmLmDZVc8tUJ-6RSMuCnC5C5sHQg3zn08Mi5okpPcCxjsB-DuIEqzVXlWvf6FnZbee0JsXgTtsRFirC1-MDcBWnFuAN3BDz64JHYCFeqHwf9cfSNgOPgs545~4EJqmBNlOH1ddJY5oNGGtmK-wxXEET-uFNF~7H9QKBPibw3gd6y3hkr5MjdAV2sR5we7RjtEqfCj2elCAXUbVy~GyKvZEEoyZZhQLD13hBc~bQdA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq048.jpeg\" /> (1 477 928 degrees of freedom).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082741 id=\"17082741\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec13>6 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In the previous section, we have shown how we can determine the physical parameters of the cluster by fitting the available data, relying on the HE equation and on a DM model that is based on the robust results of hydrodynamical cluster simulations. In this section, we present our results and we discuss their main implications. We particularly focus on the implications of our analysis for the determination of the full triaxiality, the viability of the CDM scenario, the presence of non-thermal pressure and the gas properties in the outskirts.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082743 id=\"17082743\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec14>6.1 Model parameters</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The model parameters are summarized in Table <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#t2\" class=\"figlink\">2</a></sup>. Our work indicates that Abell 1835 is a triaxial galaxy cluster with DM halo axial ratios \u03b7<sub>DM, a</sub> = 0.59 \u00b1 0.05 and \u03b7<sub>DM, b</sub> = 0.71 \u00b1 0.08, and with the major axis slightly inclined with respect to the line of sight of \u03b8 = 18.3 \u00b1 5.2 deg. Our findings give support to this view of a triaxial cluster elongated along the line of sight and they agree with the predictions of Oguri &amp; Blandford (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib53\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib53\" data-open=\"bib53\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), who have shown that SL clusters with the largest Einstein radii constitute a highly biased population with major axes preferentially aligned with the line of sight, thus increasing the magnitude of the lensing signal.</p>                        <a id=\"17082745\" scrollto-destination=\"17082745\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"t2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Table 2</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-tbl002.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=CpI-CuIqRVA2z6-hIISvQVBx7z1Z~0-WoFC~T9fZZJqiDVFvyq~kMuIMq~BqtqvsPHgvg3b6fLk3NVPUJ6tVXGpj3IEEtKu0PZN8ufqOaoN6nf~5qGO0to8gnDJprvfh0V92RHOZwbKVjBYYXDwJHk8En92SXK-ENNVHDjMpdmXGl~T7tz5ZtVqUSDuWH6nC6UsKJOjO0kZeHKx9Fo12PQ9BcyUzNTUp9x~cHB4CH7r~oFucI3S9txwJp3qQe2pa3sQdlqAYNgNtbrJPAetCrhLaRwiq1KEsy3EE0-jYs2zEhxwOede1O0MuxPNL2IV6yJT7SHM5yD7VueusWYwlhQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Model parameters of Abell 1835.
