Rows 9-14 give the model parameters <em>n</em><sub>0</sub>, <em>r</em><sub><em>c</em><sub>1</sub></sub>, \u03b5, \u03b4, <em>r</em><sub><em>c</em><sub>2</sub></sub> and \u03c5 of the IC gas density (equation 7). Rows 15 and 16 give the model parameters \u03be and <em>n</em> (normalization and slope, respectively) of the non-thermal pressure (equation 6). Finally, the last row gives the model parameter <em>P</em> of the pressure at <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, which is a boundary condition of the generalized HE equation (equation 5).</p></div></div><div content-id=\"t2\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Table 2</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-tbl002.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=CpI-CuIqRVA2z6-hIISvQVBx7z1Z~0-WoFC~T9fZZJqiDVFvyq~kMuIMq~BqtqvsPHgvg3b6fLk3NVPUJ6tVXGpj3IEEtKu0PZN8ufqOaoN6nf~5qGO0to8gnDJprvfh0V92RHOZwbKVjBYYXDwJHk8En92SXK-ENNVHDjMpdmXGl~T7tz5ZtVqUSDuWH6nC6UsKJOjO0kZeHKx9Fo12PQ9BcyUzNTUp9x~cHB4CH7r~oFucI3S9txwJp3qQe2pa3sQdlqAYNgNtbrJPAetCrhLaRwiq1KEsy3EE0-jYs2zEhxwOede1O0MuxPNL2IV6yJT7SHM5yD7VueusWYwlhQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Model parameters of Abell 1835. Rows 1-8 give the model parameters: C (concentration parameter), Rs (scale radius), \u03b3 (inner DM slope), \u03b7DM,a (minor-major axial ratio), \u03b7DM,b (intermediate.major axial ratio) and \u03c8, \u03b8, \u03c6 (Euler angles) of the DM halo (equation 2). Rows 9-14 give the model parameters n0, rc1, \u03b5, \u03b4, rc2 and \u03c5 of the IC gas density (equation 7). Rows 15 and 16 give the model parameters \u03be and n (normalization and slope, respectively) of the non-thermal pressure (equation 6). Finally, the last row gives the model parameter P of the pressure at R200, which is a boundary condition of the generalized HE equation (equation 5).\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-tbl002.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/17082745/425-3-2069-tbl002.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-tbl002.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"17082745\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/425-3-2069-tbl002.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&Signature=NZLSh3X9y1arxw2AqH5uTFUt9-ojn7dF~ZadSPNV6pCOK3TfORlr8x0N04C3-hPjBLsOt9RmVoQ1b-AXyvm6Nw72nMnpc~4ZW17n8-Dbzdev~SFHLHsZhR7glN1OtQhfZ-3Z9cRfR3M9pw9A9XuR9RUh7TSr6ggFGPmUQ728DAhEfGvt455NtgNiyTmfuNB8O55z6Ts8AGMNEj8Oqq1~X0CqmST2HEttKUjCZ3OT4Y5XjCm5h7OVS1sbzpEKF8vGdySOzFxVGnQaiwvO~rNvVJYBqRqvPGtbZDiL2gwMVhtsONYKFeFBgNLvd3u-TnEdekzJ5uv55eiAJBpB1YGyHQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=17082745&ar=982901&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-tbl002.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Model parameters of Abell 1835. Rows 1-8 give the model parameters: <em>C</em> (concentration parameter), <em>R</em><sub>s</sub> (scale radius), \u03b3 (inner DM slope), \u03b7<sub>DM,a</sub> (minor-major axial ratio), \u03b7<sub>DM,b</sub> (intermediate.major axial ratio) and \u03c8, \u03b8, \u03c6 (Euler angles) of the DM halo (equation 2). Rows 9-14 give the model parameters <em>n</em><sub>0</sub>, <em>r</em><sub><em>c</em><sub>1</sub></sub>, \u03b5, \u03b4, <em>r</em><sub><em>c</em><sub>2</sub></sub> and \u03c5 of the IC gas density (equation 7). Rows 15 and 16 give the model parameters \u03be and <em>n</em> (normalization and slope, respectively) of the non-thermal pressure (equation 6). Finally, the last row gives the model parameter <em>P</em> of the pressure at <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, which is a boundary condition of the generalized HE equation (equation 5).</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The axial ratio of the gas is \u03b7<sub>gas, a</sub> \u223c 0.77\u20130.86 and \u03b7<sub>gas, b</sub> \u223c 0.79\u20130.87, moving from the centre towards the virial radius.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The value of the virial radius is <em>R</em><sub>200</sub> = 3809 \u00b1 254 kpc. Note that we have used a triaxial definition of <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, which refers to the major axis (roughly along the line of sight) of the triaxial DM halo (see equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq004\"></span><a href=\"#eq004\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>). In order to make a comparison with a scalelength on the plane of the sky, we should multiply <em>R</em><sub>200</sub> by \u223c\u03b7<sub>DM, a</sub> (i.e. the virial radius corresponds to a scalelength of \u223c\u03b7<sub>DM, a</sub><em>R</em><sub>200</sub> \u2248 2240 kpc). This also means that, given the SZ measurements out to \u223c2200 kpc, SZ constrains the IC gas out to \u223c<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Another main result of our work is the estimate of the non-thermal pressure support, at a level up to \u223c20 per cent in the outer volumes (\u223c<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In order to assess the importance of SZ data, we have performed the following test. We excluded the SZ data from the joint analysis, fixing <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq049.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=pLDqNTSSMH0j-~RxvsyNaxBw-4Oifcvky4ctKWP~dlEeTUWJfAJt9mfzvz5h0fQ~oTgn0I0J3bSTz0XC-4CokI-XRjrfSoYjfx~-SWg0HJPlomq4K2H1k5KU48ZL8OMUAPQmpL6reFRL3CnTQbmc~EtvH-0vKw~3UgT05AR5olw0JfK8hUPXzYWLZdMdFQNSfpgnbPt3ReBtC-lSFWX2MseXLpRe4fwLDXescQ8J3kuqGQ6g1HIFwpJkGVpftWjk-GBvWdPMuzlz4c0QlZj6~cUuCae1njSbQ7IjcQke7CrVv1P7o3cf6XOwtacg2bSmbigFNhRqxMo587IN1QsUCA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq049.jpeg\" /> to the pressure at the X-ray boundary and assuming the model of <em>n</em><sub>e</sub> employed by Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). This different modelling of <em>n</em><sub>e</sub> is needed because <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq050.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=Ubs6cFCAQjEK0ZEfT9rVF3~vtla63STL9jkJEg-sl9pDxurGVU4uJC~E~TNMQ3GhE2mwo3lBzm4lzxz6d-hmAJaI5BrUah3jl4K9JHsSeQXI27HM1ydwuF7dLeaU1TMwVHbydjGfOBmaHfgzxK1iZctvdt1ASRB8Fl79jxNM9FP3xRuRzxTBtLIoto~wdxnZGByVKgab94tJ4Me91VPXCJGw2vlvWI~3DGQCojwt9JOZ7Lx560gBVR8sr3ex7L-CXL7jZ68aQ9bW4sdFxVhBHCQmCUI455HSRctMETHuYOCprpdScg313odwIFif~9gufRk29hSF153Ix~wo5W88SQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq050.jpeg\" />, \u03c5 and <em>n</em> are very poorly constrained without the SZ data sets. We have obtained larger (10\u201325 per cent) errors on the final parameters than in the case where we include SZ data. From this test, it is clear how important SZ data are to remove degeneracy among the parameters, and how crucial in measuring the physical properties of the IC gas in the outskirts, which are inaccessible to X-ray and SL observations. Therefore, in the present analysis we need to jointly combine all the data sets (X-ray, SZ and lensing) to determine the desired physical parameters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We stress that, in the internal regions, the physical properties of the cluster are overconstrained by our data (e.g. the thermal pressure is measured directly from the SZ data and also by the combination of X-ray density and spectroscopic temperature). This provides critical insight into our understanding of clusters, and critical tests of current models for the formation and evolution of galaxy clusters. Yet, in the outskirts, we mainly rely on the SZ data, because the outer volumes are not constrained by X-ray and SL observations. As a note of caution, we point out that the physical properties in the outskirts are then no longer overconstrained. While the SZ data are well described by our model, the addition of other constraints, such as weak lensing (WL) data and/or <em>Suzaku</em> X-ray data, might help us to gauge the impact of potential systematics on the desired properties in the outer volumes.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig. <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#fig003\" class=\"figlink\">003</a></sup> presents the joint probability distribution among different parameters in our triaxial model. For example, we point out that there is a positive correlation between \u03be and \u03b7<sub>DM, a</sub> (i.e. the X-ray/lensing mass discrepancy in clusters with prominent SL features can be explained by a combination of both triaxiality and non-thermal support). We have also tried to gauge the impact of possible systematics on the inferred physical parameters. For example, the choice of using the dPIE mass distribution for lensing-only data (which constrains the two-dimensional mass out to \u223c300 kpc) might affect, in principle, the derived parameters (e.g. the non-thermal component, which is constrained by the data out to \u223c<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>). In order to test this assumption, we have also fitted the lensing-only data with a gNFW model, and we have found a projected mass profile that is consistent with that derived from a dPIE mass distribution within a few per cent. Actually, the small range constrained by the SL data does not allow us to discriminate between a dPIE and a gNFW profile. This means that the actual systematic uncertainty on the physical parameters does not depend on the assumed model of DM for lensing-only data (dPIE or gNFW), but mostly stems from the calibration uncertainties of X-ray and SZ data. For X-ray and Bolocam SZ data, the calibration uncertainties have been estimated at \u223c6 per cent (Reese et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and \u223c5 per cent (Sayers et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib65\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib65\" data-open=\"bib65\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), respectively. We repeated the MCMC analysis including the previous systematics in the X-ray and SZ data (assuming that they have Gaussian distributions) and we found that the errors on the inferred parameters become slightly larger (\u223c10\u201320 per cent).</p>                        <a id=\"17082752\" scrollto-destination=\"17082752\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig003\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-fig003.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=ym~DYFvqX5iaIHLaKqyZIlPUvZ9i2PERoU0VNrbnX6dcuuI8oCV7Tg59mm7Q6YO4Wggk-93EPcoXPj9YeHP1Y5~qmVxZ3g7HL5bmhV9laJtRPfAjZ1SnMYeKn9GGFwpYM5KnPDXZHFonYILPtuu5Kw1Hf1z6ozUIXzgmLmhyEisDHOYBrS5qTW2y~3mS01SHZVMSZJNGbcGndO2v2FSIj8251CNrvxIS0DpTxrPGhDD-apmDZk4ZzB08nxlEgeW-h541GSiajc2og87WiRMIh7m-avaZM7I~D6UKfZv3vJKjcGhKnHYHKQsyrfrVItOyv0MOgJ1pzNxlaRyofkKPQw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Marginal probability distribution among different parameters in our triaxial model. Note the good agreement. For the method to recover <em>e</em> and <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq053.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=DeGvMPczOlCU8pmiyBzemC5K6Dvp9WUm75soS7oGSblBRHQ1RW7SxeUh7ntR5e~HFFunkadai14hmdt1H4EdV-xthWCE~Qj-vwHvKXg~V-lwGZyNtJDi9MGs7bK5prhlJXZcu2MCUa4wzbL1lhBevlvxiXXl97Uh2Mt7~NNCzUqZrkOBi3pvYhrtQNwo42JfdE5QdYIs-uyZzRdQbEaVC-6IOOxSQjAoY1X012TrgM~ejkWqWr9vc0Q0Kh5gXs9TW46v85tCMyrvscwAdR8TMwTqbWtR59QXO~2~LAPjf3jxofq3BnezEs6qL66jC3UYs-YC9xGGvkwLigCQeqz06Q__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq053.jpeg\" />, we refer the reader to Morandi et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082754 id=\"17082754\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec15>6.2 Implications for the viability of the CDM scenario</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Clusters are an optimal place to test the predictions of cosmological simulations regarding the mass profile of DM haloes. A key prediction arising from simulations of cosmic structure formation in a hierarchical, DM-dominated universe is that the density profile of DM haloes is universal across a wide range of mass scales, from dwarf galaxies to clusters of galaxies (Navarro, Frenk &amp; White <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49\" data-open=\"bib49\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Within a scale radius, <em>R</em><sub>s</sub>, the DM density asymptotes to a shallow power-law trend, \u03c1<sub>DM</sub>(<em>R</em>) \u221d <em>R</em><sup>\u2212\u03b3</sup>, with \u03b3 = 1, steepening at increasing radii. Simulations also suggest that galaxy cluster concentrations, which are a measure of a halo's central density, decrease gradually with virial mass. Nevertheless, the value of the logarithmic inner slope \u03b3 and the actual mass\u2013concentration relation are still debated.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Recently, in investigations of the mass distributions of individual galaxy clusters, high concentration parameters, very large Einstein radii and high efficiency in generating giant arcs have been measured. Thus, there is a major inconsistency with the theoretical CDM expectations (Broadhurst et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Limousin et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Zitrin et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib82\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib82\" data-open=\"bib82\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Moreover, measurements of \u03b3 over various mass scales have proved to be controversial, yielding conflicting values of \u03b3, with large scatter from one cluster to another. Shallow cusps have been inferred by the analysis of Sand et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib60\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib60\" data-open=\"bib60\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), raising doubts on the predictions of the CDM scenario. However, these determinations rely on the standard spherical modelling of galaxy clusters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The disagreement between theory and observation can be explained by triaxiality. In clusters that are elongated along the line of sight, the measured concentration parameter is substantially biased up with respect to the theoretical expectations, and the observed lensing properties are boosted (Meneghetti et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Moreover, elongation/flattening of the sources along the line of sight, as well as the degeneracy of \u03b3 with other parameters (i.e. the concentration parameter and the scale radius) likely affect the estimated values of \u03b3. Therefore, knowledge of the intrinsic shape and orientation of haloes is crucial for unbiased determinations of the inner slope of the DM and concentration parameter, and hence for the assessment of the viability of the standard cosmological framework (Morandi et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">One of the main results of the work presented here is a measurement of the central slope of the DM \u03b3 = 1.01 \u00b1 0.06 using a triaxial joint analysis for Abell 1835. This value agrees with the CDM predictions from Navarro et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49\" data-open=\"bib49\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) (i.e. \u03b3 = 1). We point out that we have removed the central 25 kpc in the lensing data, to avoid contamination from the cD galaxy, although we have checked that there is very little dependence of the physical parameters in the radius of the masked region. The value of the concentration parameter <em>C</em> = 4.32 \u00b1 0.44 is in agreement with the theoretical expectation from <em>N</em>-body simulations of Neto et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and Duffy et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), where <em>C</em> \u223c 4 at the redshift and for the virial mass of Abell 1835, and with an intrinsic scatter of \u223c20 per cent. Using a lensing-only analysis assuming spherical geometry, we infer a large value of the concentration parameter <em>C</em> = 6.19 \u00b1 0.63. This is above the standard <em>C</em>\u2013<em>M</em> relation, and it conflicts with the predictions of the standard model. This confirms our insights about the role of the effects of geometry on the cluster concentrations.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Given that numerical simulations customarily retrieve the concentration parameter by using a spherically averaged total density profile, we wonder whether a comparison of such simulations with the concentration parameter inferred in our triaxial framework is tenable. Thus, we have generated an ideal ellipsoidal NFW cluster with the parameters fixed to those of Abell 1835 (we fixed \u03b3 = 1 for simplicity in the comparison with simulations). Then, we measured <em>C</em> by spherically averaging the total density profile. We have found that such a concentration parameter is slightly lower (\u223c4 per cent) than the value in the triaxial framework. Given that this bias is much smaller than the intrinsic scatter of <em>C</em> in numerical simulations (\u223c20 per cent; see Neto et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), we conclude that a comparison of our findings with simulations is still convincing.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Also, we report the value of the concentration parameter for an X-ray-only analysis under the assumption of spherical geometry, <em>C</em> = 4.40 \u00b1 0.23.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Morandi et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) have analysed the galaxy clusters Abell 1689, Abell 383 and MACS J1423.8+2404 in our triaxial framework. We have found that \u03b3 and <em>C</em> are close to the CDM predictions for these clusters, in agreement with the results in the present paper. Our findings provide further evidence that supports the CDM scenario.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We also find that our inferred value of the concentration parameter and the axial ratios agree very well with those of Corless et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), who constrained the triaxial shape of the total mass distribution of Abell 1835 using weak lensing data and under a range of Bayesian priors derived from theory.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082764 id=\"17082764\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec16>6.3 Non-thermal gas pressure</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Hydrodynamical simulations have shown that a significant fraction of the total energy of the IC gas is non-thermal, mainly because of random gas motions and turbulence in the same IC gas. This energy is sourced by several mechanisms: plasma instabilities, mergers and subcluster assembly in the hierarchical structure formation scenario, shock waves, wakes of galaxies moving into the IGM, outflows from active galactic nuclei (AGNs) hosted in the centre of galaxy clusters and galactic winds (Norman &amp; Bryan <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib52\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib52\" data-open=\"bib52\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). In particular, in the hierarchical structure formation scenario, turbulent motions should occur in the IC gas while the matter continues to accrete along filaments. Indeed, gas accreting on to clusters of galaxies has bulk velocities of about <em>v</em> = 1900(<em>T</em>/6.7)<sup>.52</sup> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> at 1 Mpc (see Miniati et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Here, <em>T</em> is the gas temperature. This velocity is comparable to the expected sound speed of 1000\u20131500 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> of the ICM, and hence it generates turbulent gas motions at the boundary between the bulk flow and the thermalized ICM (Vazza et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib75\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib75\" data-open=\"bib75\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). This non-hydrostatic energy should then cascade from large to small scales and it can eventually dissipate into the gas, leading to a (partial) thermalization of the IC gas. Yet, the total energy budget \u2013 in the form of turbulent motions inside galaxy clusters, as well as their distribution and their connection with cluster dynamics and the non-gravitational process in galaxy clusters \u2013 is still open to debate in the literature.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">While the level of non-thermal pressure is typically found to be small in the central regions of clusters, it increases with radius, becoming a significant fraction of the total pressure in the outer volumes (Lau et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). It is also clear that non-thermal pressure support causes a systematic underestimate of the cluster mass recovered under the assumption of strict HE (Nagai et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Meneghetti et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). This translates into biases in the determination of the cosmological parameters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Some observational evidence of non-thermal pressure has also been published in the last few years. Schuecker et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib66\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib66\" data-open=\"bib66\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) obtained spatially resolved gas pressure maps of the Coma cluster, which indicated the presence of a significant amount of turbulence, with a spectrum of the fluctuations consistent with Kolmogorov turbulence. This yielded a lower limit of \u223c10 per cent of the total IC gas pressure in turbulent form. Additional evidence of turbulent motions inside nearby galaxies comes from the observations of pressure fluctuations in Abell 754 (Henry, Finoguenov &amp; Briel <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and Perseus (Fabian et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). The observational results of Mahdavi et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), based on X-ray and WL mass determinations, indicate that there is a radial trend of the X-ray/WL mass ratio, which is interpreted as caused by non-thermality increasing towards the outer regions. Nevertheless, their findings hinge on the assumed spherical geometry, so they have not disentangled the effect of triaxiality from non-thermal pressure support. Morandi &amp; Limousin (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) measured the non-thermal component of the gas (\u223c10 per cent) relative to the total energy budget of the inner volumes of the IC gas of Abell 383.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Motivated by the need to study the magnitude and gradient of the non-thermal pressure support, and given the constraints on the IC gas provided by SZ data out to \u223c<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, here we have implemented a model where <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub> is the fraction of the total pressure <em>P</em><sub>tot</sub>. We set this fraction to be a power law with the radius. The theoretical work of Shaw et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), based on 16 simulated clusters, has shown that this model reasonably reproduces the trend of <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub>/<em>P</em><sub>tot</sub> throughout a cluster. They found a slope of 0.8 \u00b1 0.25, in agreement with our measured value, and a normalization<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><!----></a> &gt;<em>rsim</em> 0.3, in marginal (\u223c2\u03c3) disagreement with our analysis, where we have found a normalization \u03be = 0.177 \u00b1 0.065. Therefore, our findings indicate that the level of turbulence in the numerical simulations might be overestimated.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We stress that this is the first observational measurement of the non-thermal pressure out to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, recovered using a triaxial joint X-ray, SZ and lensing analysis. Thus, our results can provide an anchor for the numerical models of ICM physics and for simulations of the formation and ongoing growth of galaxy clusters, given that measurements of the non-thermal energy in the IC gas are a proxy of the amount of energy injected into clusters from mergers, hierarchical assembly of substructures, accretion of material or feedback from AGNs. In this perspective, the cooling\u2013pre-heating simulations of Stanek et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib72\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib72\" data-open=\"bib72\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) suggest that the IC gas in the gravitation-only simulations develops more substructures with time than in the former. The suppression of substructures caused by the pre-heating leads to a lower level of kinetic energy in bulk motions with respect to simulations without pre-heating, confirming that the level of non-thermal pressure is sensitive to the particulars of the physical processes. Therefore, a more extensive physical treatment, which incorporates further physical processes in the ICM that are currently uncertain [e.g. galaxy and supermassive black hole formation, magnetohydrodynamics, viscosity, conduction, star formation feedback, magnetic fields and non-thermal plasmas, and (pre)-heating], might be needed (or improved) in simulations in order to match the amount of non-thermal energy with our observational findings.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Moreover, given that hydrodynamical simulations indicate that the non-thermal pressure provided by gas motions significantly modifies the ICM profiles in the cluster outskirts (Lau et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), the non-thermal pressure must be accurately determined in order to obtain unbiased measurements of the physical parameters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">As a note of caution, we remind the reader that our results were obtained for just one galaxy cluster, although it is expected that the observed physical processes are common, at least for relaxed objects. Thus, we plan to collect data for a larger sample of clusters to strengthen the statistical significance of our findings.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We also report on the work of Sanders et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), who placed a direct limit on turbulence based on the non-thermal velocity broadening measured from the emission lines originating in the central 30 kpc of Abell 1835. They have found that the ratio of turbulent to thermal energy density in the core is less than 13 per cent, in agreement with our present work.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=17082773 id=\"17082773\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec17>6.4 Physical properties in the outskirts</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The outskirts of galaxy clusters present an opportunity to study the formation of large-scale structure as it happens. They have special importance in cluster cosmology, because they are believed to be much less prone to complicated cluster astrophysics, such as radiative gas cooling, star formation and energy injection from AGNs, although they are potentially more susceptible to the turbulence and bulk flows that result from structure formation processes (Section <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec16\"></span><a href=\"#sec16\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>). The physics of the IC gas in the outer volumes is relatively simple and nearly self-similar, being dominated by the gravity-driven collisionless dynamics of DM and hydrodynamics of the gas. However, until very recently, observational studies of the ICM have been limited to radii that are considerably smaller than the virial radius of clusters. Here, we aim to further the understanding of the properties of the ICM in the outskirts of Abell 1835, by comparing our findings with the results of hydrodynamical numerical simulations.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Our spherically averaged gas density has good agreement with the predictions from hydrodynamical numerical simulations, including cooling, star formation and supernovae feedback (Roncarelli et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), although with a slightly flatter slope. A possible explanation for this trend is that in the above-mentioned simulation Roncarelli et al. did not include AGN feedback and/or pre-heating, which might be important, even at large radii, for smoothing the accretion pattern and leading to a flatter gas distribution (Borgani et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">It is interesting to point out that some recent <em>Suzaku</em> observations have indicated shallow density/entropy profiles in cluster outskirts, at variance with the results from previous <em>ROSAT</em> observations (Vikhlinin, Forman &amp; Jones <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib76\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib76\" data-open=\"bib76\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Eckert et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), and with the results from numerical simulations (Roncarelli et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Thus, the behaviour of the gas density in cluster outskirts is still the subject of debate. In this context, Simionescu et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib69\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib69\" data-open=\"bib69\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) and George et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) have analysed <em>Suzaku</em> X-ray observations along narrow arms, and they have found that the electron density decreases steadily with radius, approximately following a power-law model. On the contrary, we observe a general trend of steepening in the radial profiles of the gas density beyond 0.2 <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, with a logarithmic slope of \u223c2\u20132.3 in the range (0.3\u20131) <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. Eckert et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) have performed a stacking of the density profiles of a sample of clusters observed through <em>ROSAT</em> in order to analyse the outskirts of clusters, although they have not been able to determine any spectral information (i.e. the gas temperature) from these data. Their average density profile steepens beyond <em>R</em><sub>500</sub>, in agreement with the present work and with previous works by <em>ROSAT</em> (Vikhlinin et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib76\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib76\" data-open=\"bib76\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Eckert et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) have also argued that the shallow density profiles observed in some clusters by <em>Suzaku</em> might be induced by observations in preferential directions (e.g. along filaments) and that they do not reflect the typical behaviour of the outer regions of clusters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">It is also interesting to observe that the normalization of the average density near the virial radius from Eckert et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) is about 50 per cent higher than in the present analysis, where we have found a very good agreement with the predictions from the simulations of Roncarelli et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Unlike SZ data, X-ray brightness is biased by dense, cold clumps in the outer volumes, as it is traced by the square of the gas density, therefore boosting the same gas density.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We also observe a good agreement of our slope of the temperature with the theoretical predictions of Roncarelli et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) out to 0.7 <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, although near <em>R</em><sub>200</sub> our temperature profile is steeper, which suggests the presence of cold, clumpy gas.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Now we focus on the entropy profile <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq054.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=V0aIwfYqQG4IgkSfa8lbDJlxPQQRtTHfflAy0YOo9OHSKc6rVwlleTnkwadQTJE1kLnVJL5bczhBxe1BkKHR0-efLSIUEr8uBmGCREXX1Mu2eZHOiertgCJX7wmrbmnYUvQT9XPcF578pkJtiRux07JC6v4pY~v~~KD6F2r-lwvLDvLxDNsLlzCsqdPOFEgmRaVZhY2d8dYl6QXRjZ9ULGbJOwKSNkMvWsO7NnyesoRc7DG-gjLYi0rRWMnVdLwyPFTqOqHYJ2Br87BHuKp8gZrqHSfgjdLmeYyEIFmipc-bPMH5AQlNeOg9hlWsF~dAs-MCi999yNoRoo3OTlHD~Q__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq054.jpeg\" />, because this is a powerful tool to trace the thermal history of the ICM. Indeed, the gas entropy records the thermodynamic history of the ICM as the product of both gravitational and non-gravitational processes, shaping its observed structure accordingly (Voit <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib77\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib77\" data-open=\"bib77\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). The measurements of the gas entropy in the inner regions (0.1 <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>) show that the observed value of <em>S</em> is higher than that expected from the adiabatic scenario (Ponman, Cannon &amp; Navarro <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib56\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib56\" data-open=\"bib56\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), which includes only gravity. This excess in the entropy (labelled entropy \u2018floor\u2019 or \u2018ramp\u2019), with respect to the prediction of the adiabatic model, calls for some energetic mechanism, in addition to gravity, such as (pre)-heating and cooling (Bryan <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Borgani et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Morandi &amp; Ettori <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Somehow, these non-gravitational processes intervene to break the expected self-similarity of the IC gas in the innermost regions. Nevertheless, in the outer volumes, simple theoretical models predict that the entropy <em>S</em> should be self-similar and that it should behave as a power law with radius. Models of shock-dominated spherical collapse show that matter is shock-heated as it falls into clusters under the pull of gravity, with a slope of \u223c1.1 (Tozzi &amp; Norman <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib74\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib74\" data-open=\"bib74\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). In this paper, we aim to compare the theoretical predictions with the observed entropy profile out to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">It is interesting to point out that our entropy profile (see Fig. <sup class=\"sup-zero\"><a href=\"#fig004\" class=\"figlink\">004</a></sup>) roughly follows this expected trend for <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq055.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=cH2XWWzqXSE9X0h-QL9YpJjNoC-cuDmziy7FkWQUxxiISJzwlNYPq-qBGfQUKbdHGu1mN6eE3x1GN5CPHlmB375aCiCdeUulQ9~8YkIpEizMRI--6LWfyfc31m9~ljBJNegS6qT2GfR383bgOVxybs5rdtEn9CGpTZAE-6Kdtv4L9prNlkgezF3iCqhO9M-9VZiVk4gDQuahSoKLdZoFdsdrxCjrgdIEmyLQVpRbWINOENgmob6RDJn4OkXGVgVWl44r-zZsJheMIia9RHH17V~q8-AuzdeN69nYxHeOSJ0yiZpwIwLq20yL1UoMMCdVkndF0G~tl4mnapW5vj-CVg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq055.jpeg\" /> with a logarithmic slope of \u223c1. In particular, in this spatial range, we find good agreement with the adiabatic predictions of Voit et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib78\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib78\" data-open=\"bib78\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) by considering smoothed particle hydrodynamics (SPH) simulations through the <span class=\"small-caps\">gadget</span> code (Springel, Yoshida &amp; White <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib71\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib71\" data-open=\"bib71\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Springel &amp; Hernquist <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib70\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib70\" data-open=\"bib70\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>), where the entropy is defined as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"eq021\" content-id=\"eq021\"><div class=\"fig-graphic\"><div class=\"jumplink-placeholder\" id=\"425-3-2069-eq021.jpeg\"><img class=\"contentFigures\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-eq021.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=Gs~ru27sq6b4eUn1LT~NAyHDUjU30wC3snr4amdC1HvrWT5u3O48bWyFZqW9t6tPFWkbh5gyoONXyE-1ErKCDu2znu-tsbreLTHoimj0Ir~aK~veKv06STjR4W9i~bCbW8bD7RqrLMtI4oSpxVE6NAR1fhreG9ddpI2FyARyqUlbLWIW6yD8zRHWO4pmk98~WnNg0i0TmqfnaKk7JwvWOLIEAq3GZTEi5GbOKUFXjhTBJ7VQXiUA2SR8vv-x1DBiodCfhRL7WiipxntbOipBAz9qHLeLujUhQvqksIp3U5pKDh11eQ7aST9BRkkWqv2oEzsk5l4sabD0R0XGiPxx6A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"formula\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-eq021.jpeg\" /></div></div></div><span class=\"label\">18</span></div>Here, <em>S</em><sub>200</sub> is a characteristic value of the entropy at the overdensity of 200 (see, for example, equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-eq002\"></span><a href=\"#eq002\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a> in Voit et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib78\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib78\" data-open=\"bib78\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). Voit et al. also consider semi-analytical models by using clusters simulated by the AMR code <span class=\"small-caps\">enzo</span> (Norman &amp; Bryan <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib52\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib52\" data-open=\"bib52\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; O'Shea et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib55\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib55\" data-open=\"bib55\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). In this case, the normalization of the above theoretical relation is \u223c10 per cent higher than in the SPH simulations, in better agreement with our constraints. This suggests that for <em>R</em> \u22730.2 <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, the physics of the X-ray emitting gas is relatively simple and nearly self-similar, and SZ measurements can be used robustly for cosmological works.</div>                        <a id=\"17082781\" scrollto-destination=\"17082781\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig004\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/m_425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&amp;Signature=gyZKLXlWGls2~BlZIJ6r2NwUo-K-iPIi7UVuWbDPHuod4GqIyl9Wj6qSX5StEo~Gl8SYsCFur2p~zWUkuBFTbXXP6F3uiKgT8IH9RcrIn~~W0cEwjCiCJkLzdSRsQoHSe2w~TPuqhzTE90YID285Xpje~1~lEBNu1Han2bKjHBxCKWBIbvJI-nWt1H~VmJ6rVaM0aRCWFM7MCCk6Rk4dRE9GJrgmTR6dBaIPSBRwSHuISdeGwiXdLCM3APW7Y~dyYzBbqLiX3khsbklueZ2qPsif9ySzajrYJcd~fZdmBLUxWPrJ1y4a7o3OxqiSJmUkYJlKj7PSzzCAtHOfOUDmkQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Top panel: spherically averaged entropy profiles S for Abell 1835 recovered using triaxial joint X-ray, SZ and lensing analyses. The solid line represents the expectation value for S, while the 1\u03c3 errors are represented by the grey shaded region. The dashed line represents the predictions of Voit, Kay &amp; Bryan (2005), where the entropy is defined as S(R) = S200 1.32(R/R200)1.1. (2006).\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/17082781/425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"17082781\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&Signature=mrK528c4KeI7S4HhRSgzxXMgT3YaazDzRVFLZqjjTWYNj80GQJhEYmRL8935yePjBe9vWtOwY7mSmWCIMDDUgSCrugWna6erBa-HgYiwnk0~aYjkBKjQP~A9~-C8wceV9995izTtZ0EU9HKr-X9jIZGh8qXIHxf0x~qIWh2m41KaBpE-EsOHKfXud4WUOpv8XK4tOCTNzHplKwACCcer6n4uaDcZPkwC8Y94GaOxM2J~idYRkXh9nIEZPwxYadKgxSgpUg94M3vuOOXK8fnI9USD30ZKGHQRd8cGvS4~0eyC7M~qKgbp7BevzHKuhZeljgKgUJpRkmXo4NWS0v5PjA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=17082781&ar=982901&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Top panel: spherically averaged entropy profiles <em>S</em> for Abell 1835 recovered using triaxial joint X-ray, SZ and lensing analyses. The long-dashed line represents the predictions from Roncarelli et al. (2006).\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/17082781/425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"17082781\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/425/3/10.1111/j.1365-2966.2012.21196.x/2/425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg?Expires=1639265585&Signature=mrK528c4KeI7S4HhRSgzxXMgT3YaazDzRVFLZqjjTWYNj80GQJhEYmRL8935yePjBe9vWtOwY7mSmWCIMDDUgSCrugWna6erBa-HgYiwnk0~aYjkBKjQP~A9~-C8wceV9995izTtZ0EU9HKr-X9jIZGh8qXIHxf0x~qIWh2m41KaBpE-EsOHKfXud4WUOpv8XK4tOCTNzHplKwACCcer6n4uaDcZPkwC8Y94GaOxM2J~idYRkXh9nIEZPwxYadKgxSgpUg94M3vuOOXK8fnI9USD30ZKGHQRd8cGvS4~0eyC7M~qKgbp7BevzHKuhZeljgKgUJpRkmXo4NWS0v5PjA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=17082781&ar=982901&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"425-3-2069-fig004.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Top panel: spherically averaged entropy profiles <em>S</em> for Abell 1835 recovered using triaxial joint X-ray, SZ and lensing analyses. The solid line represents the expectation value for <em>S</em>, while the 1\u03c3 errors are represented by the grey shaded region. The dashed line represents the predictions of Voit, Kay &amp; Bryan (2005), where the entropy is defined as <em>S(R)</em> = <em>S</em><sub>200</sub> 1.32(<em>R/R</em>200)<sup>1.1</sup>. Here, S<sub>200</sub> is a characteristic value of the entropy at an overdensity of 200 (see, for example, equation 2 in Voit et al. 2005). Lower panel: spherically averaged gas density profile <em>n</em><sub>e</sub> for Abell 1835. The solid line represents the expectation value for ne, while the 1\u03c3 errors are represented by the grey shaded region. The long-dashed line represents the predictions from Roncarelli et al. (2006).</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Nevertheless, we observe a flattening of <em>S</em> from the power-law shape in the outskirts (<em>R</em> \u22730.8 <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>), as inferred from <em>Suzaku</em> X-ray observations (George et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>; Simionescu et al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib69\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib69\" data-open=\"bib69\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). This is perhaps indicative of infalling gas, which is not dynamically stable (Nagai &amp; Lau <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib45\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib45\" data-open=\"bib45\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>). While numerical simulations predict gas clumping in the cluster outskirts, SZ data are less biased by dense, cold clumps and gas in a multiphase state in the outer volumes with respect to X-ray data, because the SZ intensity depends linearly on the density, unlike the X-ray flux. Indeed, unlike the present analysis, previous <em>Suzaku</em> X-ray measurements point to a more pronounced flattening of <em>S</em> in the outer regions.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The gentle flattening of the entropy profile in the outskirts suggests the need for a cool phase gas and non-thermal pressure support in order to maintain dynamic stability, as already deduced in Section <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec16\"></span><a href=\"#sec16\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\"></a>. Afshordi et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) stacked <em>WMAP</em> observations of a large sample of massive clusters and found a deficit in the thermal energy in the outskirts from the SZ profile, also arguing for a cool phase of the ICM. Yet, it is not understood how these different phases would mix, and complicated gas physics are likely to result, as suggested from the marginal disagreement in the level of non-thermal pressure between simulations and the current work.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082784 id=\"17082784\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec18>7 SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we have employed a physical cluster model for Abell 1835 with a triaxial mass distribution including support from non-thermal pressure, proving that it is consistent with all the X-ray, SZ and lensing observations and the predictions of CDM models. This model relies on the following assumptions: (i) the use of a dPIE mass model for the lensing data; (ii) an assumption of generalized HE, which also accounts for the non-thermal energy contribution; (iii) the non-thermal contribution traces the thermal pressure up to a scale factor taken to be a power law with the cluster radius; (iv) the DM halo density that follows a gNFW triaxial model. We stress that, given the complementary data sets that have been included in this work, we do not need to rely on any prior from numerical simulations.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We have presented the first observational measurement of the non-thermal pressure out to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. The level of non-thermal pressure has been evaluated to be a few per cent of the total energy budget in the internal regions, while it reaches about 20 per cent in the outer volumes, a value that is lower than the predictions from numerical simulations. This indicates that an improved physical treatment in the ICM might be needed in simulations to match the amount of non-thermal energy with our observational findings. This has important consequences for estimating the amount of energy injected into clusters from mergers, the accretion of material or feedback from AGNs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We have analysed the physical properties of the IC gas in the outer volumes out to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, focusing on the entropy, which is a powerful tool for tracing the thermal history of the IC gas. We have found good agreement with the theoretical predictions, indicating that outside the innermost regions the physics of the X-ray emitting gas is relatively simple and nearly self-similar. Nevertheless, we observe that entropy tends to gently flatten in the outer volumes, which is likely to indicate infalling clumpy and cold gas that is not dynamically stable. In this context, SZ data are crucial for unbiased measurements of the cluster physical properties out to large radii, such as those presented here.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=17082788 id=\"17082788\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title js-splitscreen-backacknowledgements-title\" data-legacy-id=sec19>ACKNOWLEDGMENTS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">AM acknowledges support from the Israel Science Foundation grant 823/09.                    <h2 class=\"authorNotes-section-title\" id=\"authorNotesSectionTitle\" scrollto-destination=\"authorNotesSectionTitle\">Author notes</h2>\r\n<div content-id=\"fn1\" class=\"footnote\"><span class=\"fn\"><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">Hereafter, we equate the virial radius with <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, the radius within which the mean total density is 200 times the critical density of the Universe at the redshift of the cluster.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn2\" class=\"footnote\"><span class=\"fn\"><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\"><a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"http://www.oamp.fr/cosmology/lenstool/\" target=\"_blank\">http://www.oamp.fr/cosmology/lenstool/</a></p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn3\" class=\"footnote\"><span class=\"fn\"><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">Note that in Shaw et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\"></a>) the scale radius for <em>P</em><sub>nt</sub> refers to <em>R</em><sub>500</sub>, so we have renormalized their results to <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>.</p></div></span></div><div class=\"copyright copyright-statement\">\u00a9 2012 The Authors Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society \u00a9 2012 RAS</div><!-- /foreach -->\r\n\r\n        <span id=\"UserHasAccess\" data-userHasAccess=\"True\"></span>\r\n\r\n
